</div>
			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Hydrogen economy</h1>
									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
									<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
								<div id="contentSub"></div>
												<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
				</div>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><p>The <b>hydrogen economy</b> is a proposed system of delivering energy using <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>. The term <i>hydrogen economy</i> was coined by <a href="/wiki/John_Bockris" title="John Bockris">John Bockris</a> during a talk he gave in 1970 at <a href="/wiki/General_Motors" title="General Motors">General Motors</a> (GM) Technical Center.<sup id="cite_ref-timeline_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-timeline-1">[1]</a></sup> The concept was proposed earlier by geneticist <a href="/wiki/J.B.S._Haldane" class="mw-redirect" title="J.B.S. Haldane">J.B.S. Haldane</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>Proponents of a hydrogen economy advocate hydrogen as a potential fuel for <a href="/wiki/Motive_power" title="Motive power">motive power</a><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> (including cars and boats) and on-board auxiliary power, stationary power generation (e.g., for the energy needs of buildings), and as an energy storage medium (e.g., for interconversion from excess electric power generated off-peak). Molecular hydrogen of the sort that can be used as a fuel does not occur naturally in convenient reservoirs; nonetheless it can be generated by <a href="/wiki/Steam_reforming" title="Steam reforming">steam reformation of hydrocarbons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis_of_water" title="Electrolysis of water">water electrolysis</a> or by other methods.<sup id="cite_ref-Carmo_2013_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carmo_2013-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>The spike in attention for the concept during the 2000s has been repeatedly described as a <a href="/wiki/Promotion_(marketing)" title="Promotion (marketing)">hype</a> by <a href="/wiki/The_Hype_about_Hydrogen" title="The Hype about Hydrogen">critics</a>, neutral observers and advocates of further research and development.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Some <a href="/wiki/California_Hydrogen_Highway" title="California Hydrogen Highway">small-scale initiatives</a> from that period are still trying to enable hydrogen-fuelled land transport. However, this application faces many issues, given fundamentally low conversion efficiencies and competition from other sources.<sup id="cite_ref-Bossel_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bossel-8">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Rationale">Rationale</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Rationale">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hydrogen.economy.sys_integration_circle.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Hydrogen.economy.sys_integration_circle.jpg/300px-Hydrogen.economy.sys_integration_circle.jpg" width="300" height="299" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1f/Hydrogen.economy.sys_integration_circle.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="407" data-file-height="406" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hydrogen.economy.sys_integration_circle.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Elements of the hydrogen economy</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A hydrogen economy was proposed by the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan" title="University of Michigan">University of Michigan</a> to solve some of the negative effects of using <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbon</a> fuels where the carbon is released to the atmosphere (as carbon dioxide, carbon monoxide, unburnt hydrocarbons, etc.). Modern interest in the hydrogen economy can generally be traced to a 1970 technical report by <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_W._Jones" title="Lawrence W. Jones">Lawrence W. Jones</a> of the University of Michigan.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the current <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon_economy" title="Hydrocarbon economy">hydrocarbon economy</a>, transportation is fueled primarily by <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a>. Burning of <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">hydrocarbon fuels</a> emits <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> and other pollutants. The supply of economically usable hydrocarbon resources in the world is limited, and the demand for hydrocarbon fuels is increasing, particularly in <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" class="mw-redirect" title="People's Republic of China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, and other developing countries.</p>
<p>Proponents of a world-scale hydrogen economy argue that hydrogen can be an environmentally cleaner source of energy to end-users, particularly in transportation applications, without release of pollutants (such as particulate matter) or carbon dioxide at the point of end use. A 2004 analysis asserted that "most of the hydrogen supply chain pathways would release significantly less carbon dioxide into the atmosphere than would <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a> used in <a href="/wiki/PHEV" class="mw-redirect" title="PHEV">hybrid electric vehicles</a>" and that significant reductions in carbon dioxide emissions would be possible if <a href="/wiki/Carbon_capture" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon capture">carbon capture</a> or <a href="/wiki/Carbon_sequestration" title="Carbon sequestration">carbon sequestration</a> methods were utilized at the site of energy or hydrogen production.<sup id="cite_ref-NAS_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NAS-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hydrogen has a high <a href="/wiki/Energy_density" title="Energy density">energy density</a> by <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">weight</a> but has a low <a href="/wiki/Energy_density" title="Energy density">energy density</a> by <a href="/wiki/Volume" title="Volume">volume</a> when not highly compressed or <a href="/wiki/Liquid_hydrogen" title="Liquid hydrogen">liquified</a>. An <a href="/wiki/Otto_cycle" title="Otto cycle">Otto cycle</a> <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">internal-combustion engine</a> running on hydrogen is said to have a maximum efficiency of about 38%, 8% higher than a gasoline internal-combustion engine.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>The combination of the <a href="/wiki/Fuel_cell" title="Fuel cell">fuel cell</a> and electric motor is 2-3 times more efficient than an internal-combustion engine.<sup id="cite_ref-clarity_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-clarity-13">[13]</a></sup> However, the high capital costs of fuel cells, about $5,500/<a href="/wiki/Watt#Kilowatt" title="Watt">kW</a> in 2002,<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> are one of the major obstacles of its development, meaning that the fuel cell is only technically, but not economically, more efficient than an internal-combustion engine.<sup id="cite_ref-MIT_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MIT-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other technical obstacles include <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_storage" title="Hydrogen storage">hydrogen storage issues</a><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> and the purity requirement of hydrogen used in fuel cells – with current technology, an operating fuel cell requires the purity of hydrogen to be as high as 99.999%. On the other hand, hydrogen engine conversion technology is more economical than fuel cells.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_hydrogen_market">Current hydrogen market</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Current hydrogen market">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Realizing.the.Hydrogen.Economy.chart.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Realizing.the.Hydrogen.Economy.chart.gif/500px-Realizing.the.Hydrogen.Economy.chart.gif" width="500" height="311" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/18/Realizing.the.Hydrogen.Economy.chart.gif 1.5x" data-file-width="529" data-file-height="329" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Realizing.the.Hydrogen.Economy.chart.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Timeline</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hydrogen production is a large and growing industry. Globally, some 57 million <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">metric tons</a> of hydrogen,<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> equal to about 170&#160;million <a href="/wiki/Tonne_of_oil_equivalent" title="Tonne of oil equivalent">tons of oil equivalent</a>, were produced in 2004. The growth rate is around 10% per year. Within the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, 2004 production was about 11&#160;<a href="/wiki/Megatonne" class="mw-redirect" title="Megatonne">million metric tons</a> (Mt), an average power flow of 48&#160;<a href="/wiki/Gigawatt" class="mw-redirect" title="Gigawatt">gigawatts</a>. (For comparison, the average electric production in 2003 was some 442&#160;GW.) As of 2005, the economic value of all hydrogen produced worldwide is about $135&#160;billion per year.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are two primary uses for hydrogen today. About half is used in the <a href="/wiki/Haber_process" title="Haber process">Haber process</a> to produce <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> (NH<sub>3</sub>), which is then used directly or indirectly as <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertilizer</a>. Because both the <a href="/wiki/World_population" title="World population">world population</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Intensive_agriculture" class="mw-redirect" title="Intensive agriculture">intensive agriculture</a> used to support it are growing, ammonia demand is growing. The other half of current hydrogen production is used to convert heavy <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a> sources into lighter <a href="/wiki/Fractions" class="mw-redirect" title="Fractions">fractions</a> suitable for use as fuels. This latter process is known as <a href="/wiki/Hydrocracking" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrocracking">hydrocracking</a>. Hydrocracking represents an even larger growth area, since rising oil prices encourage oil companies to extract poorer source material, such as <a href="/wiki/Tar_sand" class="mw-redirect" title="Tar sand">tar sands</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oil_shale" title="Oil shale">oil shale</a>. The scale economies inherent in large-scale oil refining and fertilizer manufacture make possible on-site production and "captive" use. Smaller quantities of "merchant" hydrogen are manufactured and delivered to end users as well.</p>
<p>If energy for hydrogen production were available (from <a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">wind</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_energy" title="Solar energy">solar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">fission</a> or <a href="/wiki/Fusion_power" title="Fusion power">fusion nuclear power</a> etc.), use of the substance for hydrocarbon <a href="/wiki/Synfuel" class="mw-redirect" title="Synfuel">synfuel</a> production could expand captive use of hydrogen by a factor of 5 to 10. Present U.S. use of hydrogen for hydrocracking is roughly 4&#160;Mt per year. It is estimated that 37.7&#160;Mt/yr of hydrogen would be sufficient to convert enough domestic coal to liquid fuels to end U.S. dependence on foreign oil importation,<sup id="cite_ref-ANL0530Final_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ANL0530Final-21">[21]</a></sup> and less than half this figure to end dependence on Middle East oil. <a href="/wiki/Coal_liquefaction" title="Coal liquefaction">Coal liquefaction</a> would present significantly worse emissions of carbon dioxide than does the current system of burning fossil petroleum, but it would eliminate the political and economic vulnerabilities inherent in US oil importation before the commercialization of <a href="/wiki/Tight_oil" title="Tight oil">tight oil</a> in North America.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>As of 2004<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_economy&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> and 2016, 96% of global hydrogen production is from fossil fuels<sup id="cite_ref-ihs2016_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ihs2016-23">[23]</a></sup> (48% from <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a>, 30% from <a href="/wiki/Crude_oil" class="mw-redirect" title="Crude oil">oil</a>, and 18% from <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a>); water <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">electrolysis</a> accounts for only 4%.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> The distribution of production reflects the effects of thermodynamic constraints on economic choices: of the four methods for obtaining hydrogen, partial combustion of natural gas in a <a href="/wiki/NGCC" class="mw-redirect" title="NGCC">NGCC</a> (natural gas combined cycle) power plant offers the most efficient chemical pathway and the greatest off-take of usable heat energy. (needs reference)</p>
<p>The large market and sharply rising prices in fossil fuels have also stimulated great interest in alternate, cheaper means of hydrogen production.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> As of 2002, most hydrogen is produced on site and the cost is approximately $0.70/kg and, if not produced on site, the cost of liquid hydrogen is about $2.20/kg to $3.08/kg.<sup id="cite_ref-VTP_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VTP-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production.2C_storage.2C_infrastructure">Production, storage, infrastructure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Production, storage, infrastructure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_technologies" title="Hydrogen technologies">Hydrogen technologies</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_hydrogen_technologies" title="Timeline of hydrogen technologies">Timeline of hydrogen technologies</a></div>
<p>Today's hydrogen is mainly produced (&gt;90%) from <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil sources</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BHR_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BHR-28">[28]</a></sup> Linking its centralized production to a fleet of light-duty <a href="/wiki/Fuel_cell_vehicle" title="Fuel cell vehicle">fuel cell vehicles</a> would require the siting and construction of a distribution infrastructure with large investment of capital.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Further, the technological challenge of providing safe, energy-dense storage of hydrogen on board the vehicle must be overcome to provide sufficient range between fillups.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Methods_of_production">Methods of production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Methods of production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_production" title="Hydrogen production">Hydrogen production</a></div>
<p>Molecular hydrogen is not available on Earth in convenient natural reservoirs. Most hydrogen in the <a href="/wiki/Lithosphere" title="Lithosphere">lithosphere</a> is bonded to oxygen in water. Manufacturing elemental hydrogen does require the consumption of a hydrogen carrier such as a <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuel</a> or water. The former carrier consumes the fossil resource and produces carbon dioxide, but often requires no further energy input beyond the fossil fuel. <a href="/wiki/Chemical_decomposition" title="Chemical decomposition">Decomposing</a> water, the latter carrier, requires electrical or heat input, generated from some primary energy source (<a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">nuclear power</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a>). Hydrogen can also be produced by refining the effluent from geothermal sources in the <a href="/wiki/Lithosphere" title="Lithosphere">lithosphere</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Hydrogen produced by zero emission renewable energy sources such as <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis_of_water" title="Electrolysis of water">electrolysis of water</a> using <a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">wind power</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_power" title="Solar power">solar power</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydro_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydro power">hydro power</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wave_power" title="Wave power">wave power</a> or <a href="/wiki/Tidal_power" title="Tidal power">tidal power</a> is referred to as green hydrogen.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Hydrogen produced by non-renewable energy sources may be referred to as brown hydrogen. Hydrogen produced as a waste by-product or industrial by-product is sometimes referred to as grey hydrogen.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_production_methods">Current production methods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Current production methods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Hydrogen is industrially produced from <a href="/wiki/Steam_reforming" title="Steam reforming">steam reforming</a>, which uses fossil fuels such as natural gas, oil, or coal.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> The energy content of the produced hydrogen is less than the energy content of the original fuel, some of it being lost as excessive heat during production. Steam reforming leads to carbon dioxide emissions, in the same way as a car engine would do.</p>
<p>A small part (4% in 2006) is produced by <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">electrolysis</a> using electricity and water, consuming approximately 50&#160;kilowatt-hours of electricity per kilogram of hydrogen produced.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Kv.C3.A6rner-process">Kværner-process</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Kværner-process">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Kv%C3%A6rner-process" title="Kværner-process">Kværner-process</a> or Kvaerner <a href="/wiki/Carbon_black" title="Carbon black">carbon black</a> &amp; <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> process (CB&amp;H)<sup id="cite_ref-BHR_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BHR-28">[28]</a></sup> is a method, developed in the 1980s by a <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norwegian</a> <a href="/wiki/Kv%C3%A6rner" title="Kværner">company of the same name</a>, for the production of hydrogen from <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbons" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrocarbons">hydrocarbons</a> (C<sub>n</sub>H<sub>m</sub>), such as <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Biogas" title="Biogas">biogas</a>. Of the available energy of the feed, approximately 48% is contained in the hydrogen, 40% is contained in <a href="/wiki/Activated_carbon" title="Activated carbon">activated carbon</a> and 10% in superheated steam.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Biological_production">Biological production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Biological production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Biological_hydrogen_production_(Algae)" class="mw-redirect" title="Biological hydrogen production (Algae)">Biological hydrogen production (Algae)</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Fermentative_hydrogen_production" title="Fermentative hydrogen production">Fermentative hydrogen production</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Fermentation_(biochemistry)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fermentation (biochemistry)">fermentative</a> conversion of organic substrate to <a href="/wiki/Biohydrogen" title="Biohydrogen">biohydrogen</a> manifested by a diverse group <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a> using multi <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzyme</a> systems involving three steps similar to <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_digestion" title="Anaerobic digestion">anaerobic conversion</a>. <a href="/wiki/Dark_fermentation" title="Dark fermentation">Dark fermentation</a> reactions do not require light energy, so they are capable of constantly producing <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> from organic compounds throughout the day and night. <a href="/wiki/Photofermentation" title="Photofermentation">Photofermentation</a> differs from <a href="/wiki/Dark_fermentation" title="Dark fermentation">dark fermentation</a> because it only proceeds in the presence of <a href="/wiki/Light" title="Light">light</a>. For example, photo-fermentation with <i><a href="/wiki/Rhodobacter_sphaeroides" title="Rhodobacter sphaeroides">Rhodobacter sphaeroides</a></i> SH2C can be employed to convert small molecular fatty acids into hydrogen.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Electrohydrogenesis" title="Electrohydrogenesis">Electrohydrogenesis</a> is used in <a href="/wiki/Microbial_fuel_cell" title="Microbial fuel cell">microbial fuel cells</a> where hydrogen is produced from organic matter (e.g. from sewage, or solid matter <sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>) while 0.2 - 0.8 V is applied.</p>
<p>Biological hydrogen can be produced in an <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a> <a href="/wiki/Bioreactor" title="Bioreactor">bioreactor</a>. In the late 1990s it was discovered that if the algae is deprived of <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> it will switch from the production of <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, i.e. normal <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a>, to the production of hydrogen.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>Biological hydrogen can be produced in bioreactors that use feedstocks other than algae, the most common feedstock being waste streams. The process involves bacteria feeding on hydrocarbons and excreting hydrogen and CO<sub>2</sub>. The CO<sub>2</sub> can be sequestered successfully by several methods, leaving hydrogen gas. A prototype hydrogen bioreactor using waste as a feedstock is in operation at Welch's grape juice factory in North East, Pennsylvania.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Biocatalysed_electrolysis">Biocatalysed electrolysis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Biocatalysed electrolysis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Besides regular electrolysis, electrolysis using microbes is another possibility. With biocatalysed electrolysis, hydrogen is generated after running through the microbial fuel cell and a variety of <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.glastuinbouw.wur.nl/UK/expertise/energy/innovations/plantenergy/">aquatic plants</a> can be used. These include <a href="/wiki/Glyceria_maxima" title="Glyceria maxima">reed sweetgrass</a>, cordgrass, rice, tomatoes, lupines, and algae <sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Electrolysis_of_water">Electrolysis of water</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Electrolysis of water">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hydrogen-challenger_hg.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Hydrogen-challenger_hg.jpg/300px-Hydrogen-challenger_hg.jpg" width="300" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Hydrogen-challenger_hg.jpg/450px-Hydrogen-challenger_hg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Hydrogen-challenger_hg.jpg/600px-Hydrogen-challenger_hg.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2420" data-file-height="1155" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hydrogen-challenger_hg.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Electrolysis of water ship <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_Challenger" title="Hydrogen Challenger">Hydrogen Challenger</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hydrogen can be made via <a href="/wiki/High_pressure_electrolysis" class="mw-redirect" title="High pressure electrolysis">high pressure electrolysis</a>, low pressure <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis_of_water" title="Electrolysis of water">electrolysis of water</a>, or a range of other emerging electrochemical processes such as high temperature electrolysis or carbon assisted electrolysis.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Current best processes have an efficiency of 50% to 80%,<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> so that 1&#160;kg of hydrogen (which has a <a href="/wiki/Specific_energy" title="Specific energy">specific energy</a> of 143 MJ/kg, about 40 kWh/kg) requires 50–79 kWh of electricity. At $0.08/kWh (approx. $4/kg), it is with traditional methods 3 to 10 times costlier than steam reformation of natural gas.<sup id="cite_ref-VTP_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VTP-27">[27]</a></sup> The price difference is due to the efficiency of direct conversion of fossil fuels to produce hydrogen, rather than burning fuel to produce electricity.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="High-pressure_electrolysis">High-pressure electrolysis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: High-pressure electrolysis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/High_pressure_electrolysis" class="mw-redirect" title="High pressure electrolysis">High pressure electrolysis</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis_of_water" title="Electrolysis of water">electrolysis of water</a> by decomposition of <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> (H<sub>2</sub>O) into <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> (O<sub>2</sub>) and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> gas (H<sub>2</sub>) by means of an <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electric current</a> being passed through the water. The difference with a standard <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis_of_water" title="Electrolysis of water">electrolyzer</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Compressed_hydrogen" title="Compressed hydrogen">compressed hydrogen</a> output around 120-200 <a href="/wiki/Bar_(unit)" title="Bar (unit)">bar</a> (1740-2900 <a href="/wiki/Pounds_per_square_inch" title="Pounds per square inch">psi</a>, 12–20 <a href="/wiki/Megapascal" class="mw-redirect" title="Megapascal">MPa</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> By pressurising the hydrogen in the electrolyser, through a process known as chemical compression, the need for an external <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_compressor" title="Hydrogen compressor">hydrogen compressor</a> is eliminated,<sup id="cite_ref-Carmo_2013_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carmo_2013-4">[4]</a></sup> the average energy consumption for internal compression is around 3%.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> European largest (1 400 000&#160;kg/a, High-pressure Electrolysis of water, acaline technology) hydrogen production plant is operating at Kokkola, Finland.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="High-temperature_electrolysis">High-temperature electrolysis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: High-temperature electrolysis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/High-temperature_electrolysis" title="High-temperature electrolysis">High-temperature electrolysis</a></div>
<p>Hydrogen can be generated from energy supplied in the form of heat and electricity through high-temperature electrolysis (HTE). Because some of the energy in HTE is supplied in the form of heat, less of the energy must be converted twice (from heat to electricity, and then to chemical form), and so potentially far less energy is required per kilogram of hydrogen produced.</p>
<p>While nuclear-generated electricity could be used for electrolysis, nuclear heat can be directly applied to split hydrogen from water. High temperature (950–1000&#160;°C) gas cooled nuclear reactors have the potential to split hydrogen from water by thermochemical means using nuclear heat. Research into high-temperature nuclear reactors may eventually lead to a hydrogen supply that is cost-competitive with natural gas steam reforming. <a href="/wiki/General_Atomics" title="General Atomics">General Atomics</a> predicts that hydrogen produced in a High Temperature Gas Cooled Reactor (HTGR) would cost $1.53/kg. In 2003, steam reforming of natural gas yielded hydrogen at $1.40/kg. In 2005 natural gas prices, hydrogen costs $2.70/kg.</p>
<p>High-temperature electrolysis has been demonstrated in a laboratory, at 108&#160;<a href="/wiki/Megajoule" class="mw-redirect" title="Megajoule">MJ</a> (thermal) per kilogram of hydrogen produced,<sup id="cite_ref-INL_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-INL-44">[44]</a></sup> but not at a commercial scale. In addition, this is lower-quality "commercial" grade Hydrogen, unsuitable for use in fuel cells.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Photoelectrochemical_water_splitting">Photoelectrochemical water splitting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Photoelectrochemical water splitting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Using electricity produced by photovoltaic systems offers the cleanest way to produce hydrogen. Water is broken into hydrogen and oxygen by electrolysis—a <a href="/wiki/Photoelectrochemical_cell" title="Photoelectrochemical cell">photoelectrochemical cell</a> (PEC) process which is also named <a href="/wiki/Artificial_photosynthesis" title="Artificial photosynthesis">artificial photosynthesis</a>. William Ayers at Energy Conversion Devices demonstrated and patented the first multijunction high efficiency photoelectrochemical system for direct splitting of water in 1983.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> This group demonstrated direct water splitting now referred to as an "artificial leaf" or "wireless solar water splitting" with a low cost thin film amorphous silicon multijunction sheet immersed directly in water. Hydrogen evolved on the front amorphous silcon surface decorated with various catalysts while oxygen evolved off the back metal substrate. A Nafion membrane above the multijunction cell provided a path for ion transport. Their patent also lists a variety of other semiconductor multijunction materials for the direct water splitting in addition to amorphous silicon and silicon germanium alloys. Research continues towards developing high-efficiency <a href="/wiki/Multi-junction_cell" class="mw-redirect" title="Multi-junction cell">multi-junction cell</a> technology at universities and the photovoltaic industry. If this process is assisted by photocatalysts suspended directly in water instead of using photovoltaic and an electrolytic system, the reaction is in just one step, which can improve efficiency.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Concentrating_solar_thermal">Concentrating solar thermal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Concentrating solar thermal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Very high temperatures are required to dissociate water into hydrogen and oxygen. A catalyst is required to make the process operate at feasible temperatures. Heating the water can be achieved through the use of <a href="/wiki/Concentrating_solar_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Concentrating solar power">concentrating solar power</a>. <a href="/wiki/Hydrosol-2" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrosol-2">Hydrosol-2</a> is a 100-kilowatt pilot plant at the <a href="/wiki/Plataforma_Solar_de_Almer%C3%ADa" title="Plataforma Solar de Almería">Plataforma Solar de Almería</a> in <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> which uses sunlight to obtain the required 800 to 1,200&#160;°C to heat water. Hydrosol II has been in operation since 2008. The design of this 100-kilowatt pilot plant is based on a modular concept. As a result, it may be possible that this technology could be readily scaled up to the megawatt range by multiplying the available reactor units and by connecting the plant to <a href="/wiki/Heliostat" title="Heliostat">heliostat</a> fields (fields of sun-tracking mirrors) of a suitable size.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Photoelectrocatalytic_production">Photoelectrocatalytic production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Photoelectrocatalytic production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>A method studied by Thomas Nann and his team at the University of East Anglia consists of a gold electrode covered in layers of indium phosphide (InP) nanoparticles. They introduced an iron-sulfur complex into the layered arrangement, which when submerged in water and irradiated with light under a small electric current, produced hydrogen with an efficiency of 60%.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2015, it was reported that <a href="/wiki/Panasonic_Corp." class="mw-redirect" title="Panasonic Corp.">Panasonic Corp.</a> has developed a <a href="/wiki/Photocatalysis" title="Photocatalysis">photocatalyst</a> based on <a href="/wiki/Niobium_nitride" title="Niobium nitride">niobium nitride</a> that can absorb 57% of sunlight to support the <a href="/wiki/Decomposition" title="Decomposition">decomposition</a> of water to produce hydrogen gas.<sup id="cite_ref-asahi_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-asahi-51">[51]</a></sup> The company plans to achieve commercial application "as early as possible", not before 2020.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Thermochemical_production">Thermochemical production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Thermochemical production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>There are more than 352<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> thermochemical cycles which can be used for <a href="/wiki/Water_splitting" title="Water splitting">water splitting</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> around a dozen of these cycles such as the <a href="/wiki/Iron_oxide_cycle" title="Iron oxide cycle">iron oxide cycle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cerium(IV)_oxide-cerium(III)_oxide_cycle" class="mw-redirect" title="Cerium(IV) oxide-cerium(III) oxide cycle">cerium(IV) oxide-cerium(III) oxide cycle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zinc_zinc-oxide_cycle" class="mw-redirect" title="Zinc zinc-oxide cycle">zinc zinc-oxide cycle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sulfur-iodine_cycle" class="mw-redirect" title="Sulfur-iodine cycle">sulfur-iodine cycle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Copper-chlorine_cycle" class="mw-redirect" title="Copper-chlorine cycle">copper-chlorine cycle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_sulfur_cycle" title="Hybrid sulfur cycle">hybrid sulfur cycle</a> are under research and in testing phase to produce hydrogen and oxygen from water and heat without using electricity.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> These processes can be more efficient than high-temperature electrolysis, typical in the range from 35% - 49% <a href="/wiki/Lower_heating_value" class="mw-redirect" title="Lower heating value">LHV</a> efficiency. Thermochemical production of hydrogen using chemical energy from coal or natural gas is generally not considered, because the direct chemical path is more efficient.</p>
<p>None of the thermochemical hydrogen production processes have been demonstrated at production levels, although several have been demonstrated in laboratories.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydrogen_as_a_byproduct_of_other_chemical_processes">Hydrogen as a byproduct of other chemical processes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Hydrogen as a byproduct of other chemical processes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The industrial production of <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Caustic_soda" class="mw-redirect" title="Caustic soda">caustic soda</a> by <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">electrolysis</a> generates a sizable amount of Hydrogen as a byproduct. In the port of Antwerp a 1MW demonstration fuel cell power plant is powered by such byproduct. This unit has been operational since late 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> The excess hydrogen is often managed with a <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_pinch" title="Hydrogen pinch">hydrogen pinch</a> analysis.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Storage">Storage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Storage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_storage" title="Hydrogen storage">Hydrogen storage</a></div>
<p>Although molecular hydrogen has very high energy density on a mass basis, partly because of its low <a href="/wiki/Molecular_weight" class="mw-redirect" title="Molecular weight">molecular weight</a>, as a gas at ambient conditions it has very low energy density by volume. If it is to be used as fuel stored on board the vehicle, pure hydrogen gas must be stored in an energy-dense form to provide sufficient driving range.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pressurized_hydrogen_gas">Pressurized hydrogen gas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Pressurized hydrogen gas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Increasing gas pressure improves the energy density by volume, making for smaller, but not lighter container tanks (see <a href="/wiki/Pressure_vessel" title="Pressure vessel">pressure vessel</a>). Achieving higher pressures necessitates greater use of external energy to power the compression. The mass of the <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_tank" title="Hydrogen tank">hydrogen tanks</a> needed for <a href="/wiki/Compressed_hydrogen" title="Compressed hydrogen">compressed hydrogen</a> reduces the fuel economy of the vehicle. Because it is a small molecule, hydrogen tends to diffuse through any liner material intended to contain it, leading to the <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_embrittlement" title="Hydrogen embrittlement">embrittlement</a>, or weakening, of its container. The most common method of on board hydrogen storage in today's demonstration vehicles is as a compressed gas at pressures of roughly 700&#160;bar (70&#160;<a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">MPa</a>).</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Liquid_hydrogen">Liquid hydrogen</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Liquid hydrogen">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Alternatively, higher volumetric energy density <a href="/wiki/Liquid_hydrogen" title="Liquid hydrogen">liquid hydrogen</a> or <a href="/wiki/Slush_hydrogen" title="Slush hydrogen">slush hydrogen</a> may be used. However, liquid hydrogen is cryogenic and boils at 20.268&#160;K (–252.882&#160;°C or –423.188&#160;°F). <a href="/wiki/Cryogenic" class="mw-redirect" title="Cryogenic">Cryogenic</a> storage cuts weight but requires large <a href="/wiki/Liquification" class="mw-redirect" title="Liquification">liquification</a> energies. The liquefaction process, involving pressurizing and cooling steps, is energy intensive.<sup id="cite_ref-zubrin-117_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zubrin-117-56">[56]</a></sup> The liquefied hydrogen has lower energy density by volume than gasoline by approximately a factor of four, because of the low density of liquid hydrogen — there is actually more hydrogen in a liter of gasoline (116&#160;grams) than there is in a liter of pure liquid hydrogen (71&#160;grams). Liquid <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_tank" title="Hydrogen tank">hydrogen storage tanks</a> must also be well insulated to minimize boil off. Ice may form around the tank and help corrode it further if the liquid hydrogen tank insulation fails.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Storage_as_hydride">Storage as hydride</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Storage as hydride">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Distinct from storing molecular hydrogen, hydrogen can be stored as a chemical <a href="/wiki/Hydride" title="Hydride">hydride</a> or in some other hydrogen-containing compound. Hydrogen gas is reacted with some other materials to produce the hydrogen storage material, which can be transported relatively easily. At the point of use the hydrogen storage material can be made to decompose, yielding hydrogen gas. As well as the mass and volume density problems associated with molecular hydrogen storage, current barriers to practical storage schemes stem from the high pressure and temperature conditions needed for hydride formation and hydrogen release. For many potential systems hydriding and dehydriding <a href="/wiki/Chemical_kinetics" title="Chemical kinetics">kinetics</a> and heat management are also issues that need to be overcome. A French company McPhy Energy <a rel="nofollow" class="external autonumber" href="http://www.mcphy.com">[3]</a> is developing the first industrial product, based on Magnesium Hydrate, already sold to some major clients such as Iwatani and ENEL.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Adsorption">Adsorption</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Adsorption">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>A third approach is to <a href="/wiki/Adsorption" title="Adsorption">adsorb</a> molecular hydrogen on the surface of a solid storage material. Unlike in the hydrides mentioned above, the hydrogen does not dissociate/recombine upon charging/discharging the storage system, and hence does not suffer from the kinetic limitations of many hydride storage systems. Hydrogen densities similar to liquefied hydrogen can be achieved with appropriate adsorbent materials. Some suggested adsorbents include <a href="/wiki/Activated_carbon" title="Activated carbon">activated carbon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nanostructure" title="Nanostructure">nanostructured</a> carbons (including <a href="/wiki/Carbon_nanotube" title="Carbon nanotube">CNTs</a>), <a href="/wiki/Metal-Organic_Framework" class="mw-redirect" title="Metal-Organic Framework">MOFs</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_clathrate_hydrate" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrogen clathrate hydrate">hydrogen clathrate hydrate</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Underground_hydrogen_storage">Underground hydrogen storage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Underground hydrogen storage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Underground_hydrogen_storage" title="Underground hydrogen storage">Underground hydrogen storage</a> is the practice of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_storage" title="Hydrogen storage">hydrogen storage</a> in underground <a href="/wiki/Cave" title="Cave">caverns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Salt_dome" title="Salt dome">salt domes</a> and depleted oil and gas fields.<sup id="cite_ref-RSC_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RSC-57">[57]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Large quantities of gaseous hydrogen have been stored in underground caverns by <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Chemical_Industries" title="Imperial Chemical Industries">Imperial Chemical Industries</a> (ICI) for many years without any difficulties.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> The European project <a href="/w/index.php?title=Hyunder&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Hyunder (page does not exist)">Hyunder</a><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> indicated in 2013 that for the storage of wind and solar energy an additional 85 caverns are required as it can't be covered by <a href="/wiki/Pumped-storage_hydroelectricity" title="Pumped-storage hydroelectricity">pumped-storage hydroelectricity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Compressed_air_energy_storage" title="Compressed air energy storage">compressed air energy storage</a> systems.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Power_to_gas">Power to gas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Power to gas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Power_to_gas" title="Power to gas">Power to gas</a> is a technology which converts <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electrical</a> power to a gas <a href="/wiki/Fuel" title="Fuel">fuel</a>. There are 2 methods, the first is to use the electricity for <a href="/wiki/Water_splitting" title="Water splitting">water splitting</a> and inject the resulting hydrogen into the natural gas grid. The second (less efficient) method is used to convert <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> and water to <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, (see <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a>) using <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">electrolysis</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sabatier_reaction" title="Sabatier reaction">Sabatier reaction</a>. The excess power or off peak power generated by wind generators or solar arrays is then used for load balancing in the energy grid. Using the existing natural gas system for hydrogen Fuel cell maker <a href="/wiki/Hydrogenics" title="Hydrogenics">Hydrogenics</a> and natural gas distributor <a href="/wiki/Enbridge" title="Enbridge">Enbridge</a> have teamed up to develop such a <a href="/wiki/Power_to_gas" title="Power to gas">power to gas</a> system in Canada.<sup id="cite_ref-anscombe_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-anscombe-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pipeline_storage">Pipeline storage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Pipeline storage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>A natural gas network may be used for the storage of hydrogen. Before switching to <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a>, the German gas networks were operated using <a href="/wiki/Towngas" class="mw-redirect" title="Towngas">towngas</a>, which for the most part consisted of hydrogen. The storage capacity of the German natural gas network is more than 200,000 GW·h which is enough for several months of energy requirement. By comparison, the capacity of all German pumped storage power plants amounts to only about 40 GW·h. The transport of energy through a gas network is done with much less loss (&lt;0.1%) than in a power network (8%). The use of the existing <a href="/wiki/List_of_natural_gas_pipelines" title="List of natural gas pipelines">natural gas pipelines</a> for hydrogen was studied by NaturalHy<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Infrastructure">Infrastructure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Infrastructure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_infrastructure" title="Hydrogen infrastructure">Hydrogen infrastructure</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Photo_praxair_plant.hydrogen.infrastructure.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cd/Photo_praxair_plant.hydrogen.infrastructure.jpg" width="250" height="202" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="250" data-file-height="202" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Photo_praxair_plant.hydrogen.infrastructure.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Praxair Hydrogen Plant</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The hydrogen infrastructure would consist mainly of industrial <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_pipeline_transport" title="Hydrogen pipeline transport">hydrogen pipeline transport</a> and hydrogen-equipped filling stations like those found on a <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_highway" title="Hydrogen highway">hydrogen highway</a>. <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_stations" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrogen stations">Hydrogen stations</a> which were not situated near a hydrogen pipeline would get supply via <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_tank" title="Hydrogen tank">hydrogen tanks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Compressed_hydrogen_tube_trailer" title="Compressed hydrogen tube trailer">compressed hydrogen tube trailers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liquid_hydrogen_trailer" title="Liquid hydrogen trailer">liquid hydrogen trailers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liquid_hydrogen_tank_truck" class="mw-redirect" title="Liquid hydrogen tank truck">liquid hydrogen tank trucks</a> or dedicated onsite production.</p>
<p>Because of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_embrittlement" title="Hydrogen embrittlement">hydrogen embrittlement</a> of steel, and corrosion<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> natural gas pipes require internal coatings or replacement in order to convey hydrogen. Techniques are well-known; over 700 miles of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_piping" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrogen piping">hydrogen pipeline</a> currently exist in the United States. Although expensive, pipelines are the cheapest way to move hydrogen. Hydrogen gas piping is routine in large oil-refineries, because hydrogen is used to <a href="/wiki/Hydrocracking" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrocracking">hydrocrack</a> fuels from crude oil.</p>
<p>Hydrogen piping can in theory be avoided in distributed systems of hydrogen production, where hydrogen is routinely made on site using medium or small-sized generators which would produce enough hydrogen for personal use or perhaps a neighborhood. In the end, a combination of options for hydrogen gas distribution may succeed.</p>
<p>While millions of tons of elemental hydrogen are distributed around the world each year in various ways, bringing hydrogen to individual consumers would require an evolution of the fuel infrastructure. For example, according to GM, 70% of the U.S. population lives near a hydrogen-generating facility but has little public access to that hydrogen. The same study however, shows that building the infrastructure in a systematic way is much more doable and affordable than most people think. For example, one article has noted that hydrogen stations could be put within every 10 miles in metro Los Angeles, and on the highways between LA and neighboring cities like Palm Springs, Las Vegas, San Diego and Santa Barbara, for the cost of a Starbuck's latte for every one of the 15 million residents living in these areas.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="A_key_tradeoff:_centralized_vs._distributed_production">A key tradeoff: centralized vs. distributed production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: A key tradeoff: centralized vs. distributed production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In a future full hydrogen economy, primary energy sources and feedstock would be used to produce hydrogen gas as stored energy for use in various sectors of the economy. Producing hydrogen from primary energy sources other than coal, oil, and natural gas, would result in lower production of the greenhouse gases characteristic of the combustion of these fossil energy resources.</p>
<p>One key feature of a hydrogen economy would be that in mobile applications (primarily vehicular transport) energy generation and use could be decoupled. The primary energy source would need no longer travel with the vehicle, as it currently does with hydrocarbon fuels. Instead of tailpipes creating dispersed emissions, the energy (and pollution) could be generated from point sources such as large-scale, centralized facilities with improved efficiency. This would allow the possibility of technologies such as <a href="/wiki/Carbon_sequestration" title="Carbon sequestration">carbon sequestration</a>, which are otherwise impossible for mobile applications. Alternatively, <a href="/wiki/Distributed_generation" title="Distributed generation">distributed energy generation</a> schemes (such as small scale renewable energy sources) could be used, possibly associated with <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_stations" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrogen stations">hydrogen stations</a>.</p>
<p>Aside from the energy generation, hydrogen production could be centralized, distributed or a mixture of both. While generating hydrogen at centralized primary energy plants promises higher hydrogen production efficiency, difficulties in high-volume, long range hydrogen transportation (due to factors such as <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_damage" title="Hydrogen damage">hydrogen damage</a> and the ease of hydrogen diffusion through solid materials) makes electrical energy distribution attractive within a hydrogen economy. In such a scenario, small regional plants or even local filling stations could generate hydrogen using energy provided through the electrical distribution grid. While hydrogen generation efficiency is likely to be lower than for centralized hydrogen generation, losses in hydrogen transport could make such a scheme more efficient in terms of the primary energy used per kilogram of hydrogen delivered to the end user.</p>
<p>The proper balance between hydrogen distribution and long-distance electrical distribution is one of the primary questions that arises about the hydrogen economy.</p>
<p>Again the dilemmas of production sources and transportation of hydrogen can now be overcome using on site (home, business, or fuel station) generation of hydrogen from off grid renewable sources.<a rel="nofollow" class="external autonumber" href="http://itm-power.com">[4]</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Distributed_electrolysis">Distributed electrolysis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Distributed electrolysis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Distributed electrolysis would bypass the problems of distributing hydrogen by distributing electricity instead. It would use existing electrical networks to transport electricity to small, on-site electrolysers located at filling stations. However, accounting for the energy used to produce the electricity and transmission losses would reduce the overall efficiency.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">Natural gas</a> <a href="/wiki/Combined_cycle" title="Combined cycle">combined cycle</a> <a href="/wiki/Power_plants" class="mw-redirect" title="Power plants">power plants</a>, which account for almost all construction of new electricity generation plants in the United States, generate electricity at efficiencies of 60 percent or greater.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Increased demand for electricity, whether due to hydrogen cars or other demand, would have the marginal impact of adding new combined cycle power plants. On this basis, distributed production of hydrogen would be roughly 40% efficient. However, if the marginal impact is referred to today's power grid, with an efficiency of roughly 40% owing to its mix of fuels and conversion methods, the efficiency of distributed hydrogen production would be roughly 25%.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>The distributed production of hydrogen in this fashion would be expected to generate air emissions of pollutants and carbon dioxide at various points in the supply chain, e.g., electrolysis, transportation and storage. Such externalities as pollution must be weighed against the potential advantages of a hydrogen economy.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Fuel_cells_as_alternative_to_internal_combustion">Fuel cells as alternative to internal combustion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Fuel cells as alternative to internal combustion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Fuel_cell" title="Fuel cell">Fuel cell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_vehicle" title="Hydrogen vehicle">Hydrogen vehicle</a></div>
<p>One of the main offerings of a hydrogen economy is that the fuel can replace the fossil fuel burned in <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">internal combustion engines</a> and <a href="/wiki/Turbine" title="Turbine">turbines</a> as the primary way to convert chemical energy into kinetic or electrical energy; hereby eliminating greenhouse gas emissions and pollution from that engine. Although hydrogen can be used in conventional internal combustion engines, fuel cells, being <a href="/wiki/Electrochemical" class="mw-redirect" title="Electrochemical">electrochemical</a>, have a theoretical efficiency advantage over heat engines. Fuel cells are more expensive to produce than common internal combustion engines.</p>
<p>Some types of fuel cells work with hydrocarbon fuels,<sup id="cite_ref-e-collection.ethbib.ethz.ch_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-e-collection.ethbib.ethz.ch-68">[68]</a></sup> while all can be operated on pure hydrogen. In the event that fuel cells become price-competitive with internal combustion engines and turbines, large gas-fired power plants could adopt this technology.</p>
<p>Hydrogen gas must be distinguished as "technical-grade" (five times pure), which is suitable for applications such as fuel cells, and "commercial-grade", which has carbon- and sulfur-containing impurities, but which can be produced by the much cheaper steam-reformation process. Fuel cells require high-purity hydrogen because the impurities would quickly degrade the life of the fuel cell stack.</p>
<p>Much of the interest in the hydrogen economy concept is focused on the use of fuel cells to power electric <a href="/wiki/Car" title="Car">cars</a>. Current hydrogen fuel cells suffer from a low <a href="/wiki/Power-to-weight_ratio" title="Power-to-weight ratio">power-to-weight ratio</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> Fuel cells are much more efficient than internal combustion engines, and produce no harmful emissions. If a practical method of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_storage" title="Hydrogen storage">hydrogen storage</a> is introduced, and fuel cells become cheaper, they can be economically viable to power <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_vehicle" title="Hybrid vehicle">hybrid</a> fuel cell/<a href="/wiki/Electric_battery" class="mw-redirect" title="Electric battery">battery</a> vehicles, or purely fuel cell-driven ones. The economic viability of fuel cell powered vehicles will improve as the hydrocarbon fuels used in internal combustion engines become more expensive, because of the depletion of easily accessible reserves or economic accounting of environmental impact through such measures as <a href="/wiki/Carbon_tax" title="Carbon tax">carbon taxes</a>.</p>
<p>Other fuel cell technologies based on the exchange of metal ions (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Zinc-air_battery" class="mw-redirect" title="Zinc-air battery">zinc-air fuel cells</a>) are typically more efficient at energy conversion than hydrogen fuel cells, but the widespread use of any electrical energy&#160;→ chemical energy&#160;→ electrical energy systems would necessitate the production of electricity.</p>
<p>Since the 2003 State of the Union address, when the notion of the hydrogen economy came to national prominence in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, there has been a steady chorus of naysayers. Most recently, in 2013, Lux Research, Inc. issued a report that stated: "The dream of a hydrogen economy ... is no nearer." It concluded that "Capital cost, not hydrogen supply, will limit adoption to a mere 5.9 GW" by 2030, providing "a nearly insurmountable barrier to adoption, except in niche applications". Lux's analysis speculated that by 2030, PEM stationary market will reach $1 billion, while the vehicle market, including forklifts, will reach a total of $2 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-Lux2013_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lux2013-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_as_an_automotive_fuel_and_system_efficiency">Use as an automotive fuel and system efficiency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Use as an automotive fuel and system efficiency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_fuel" title="Hydrogen fuel">Hydrogen fuel</a></div>
<p>An accounting of the energy utilized during a thermodynamic process, known as an energy balance, can be applied to automotive fuels. With today's technology, the manufacture of hydrogen via <a href="/wiki/Steam_reforming" title="Steam reforming">steam reforming</a> can be accomplished with a thermal efficiency of 75 to 80&#160;percent. Additional energy will be required to liquefy or compress the hydrogen, and to transport it to the filling station via truck or pipeline. The energy that must be utilized per kilogram to produce, transport and deliver hydrogen (i.e., its well-to-tank energy use) is approximately 50&#160;<a href="/wiki/Megajoule" class="mw-redirect" title="Megajoule">MJ</a> using technology available in 2004. Subtracting this energy from the enthalpy of one kilogram of hydrogen, which is 141&#160;MJ, and dividing by the enthalpy, yields a thermal energy efficiency of roughly 60%.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Gasoline, by comparison, requires less energy input, per gallon, at the refinery, and comparatively little energy is required to transport it and store it owing to its high energy density per gallon at ambient temperatures. Well-to-tank, the supply chain for gasoline is roughly 80% efficient (Wang, 2002). Another grid-based method of supplying hydrogen would be to use <a href="/wiki/Electrical_power_industry" class="mw-redirect" title="Electrical power industry">electrical</a> to run electrolysers. Roughly 6% of electricity is lost during transmission along power lines, and the process of converting the fossil fuel to electricity in the first place is roughly 33 percent efficient.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bossel_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bossel-8">[8]</a></sup> Thus if efficiency is the key determinant it would be unlikely hydrogen vehicles would be fueled by such a method, and indeed viewed this way, <a href="/wiki/Electric_car" title="Electric car">electric vehicles</a> would appear to be a better choice. However, as noted above, hydrogen can be produced from a number of feedstocks, in centralized or distributed fashion, and these afford more efficient pathways to produce and distribute the fuel.</p>
<p>A study of the well-to-wheels efficiency of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_vehicle" title="Hydrogen vehicle">hydrogen vehicles</a> compared to other vehicles in the Norwegian energy system indicates that hydrogen fuel-cell vehicles (FCV) tend to be about a third as efficient as EVs when electrolysis is used, with hydrogen Internal Combustion Engines (ICE) being barely a sixth as efficient. Even in the case where hydrogen fuel cells get their hydrogen from natural gas reformation rather than electrolysis, and EVs get their power from a natural gas power plant, the EVs still come out ahead 35% to 25% (and only 13% for a H<sub>2</sub> ICE). This compares to 14% for a gasoline ICE, 27% for a gasoline ICE hybrid, and 17% for a diesel ICE, also on a well-to-wheels basis.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hydrogen has been called one of the least efficient and most expensive possible replacements for gasoline (petrol) in terms of reducing greenhouse gases; other technologies may be less expensive and more quickly implemented.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> A comprehensive study of hydrogen in transportation applications has found that "there are major hurdles on the path to achieving the vision of the hydrogen economy; the path will not be simple or straightforward".<sup id="cite_ref-NAS_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NAS-11">[11]</a></sup> Although <a href="/wiki/Ford_Motor_Company" title="Ford Motor Company">Ford Motor Company</a> and French <a href="/wiki/Renault-Nissan" class="mw-redirect" title="Renault-Nissan">Renault-Nissan</a> cancelled their hydrogen car R&amp;D efforts in 2008 and 2009, respectively,<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> they signed a 2009 letter of intent with the other manufacturers and Now GMBH in September 2009 supporting the commercial introduction of FCVs by 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> A study by <a href="/wiki/The_Carbon_Trust" class="mw-redirect" title="The Carbon Trust">The Carbon Trust</a> for the UK <a href="/wiki/Department_of_Energy_and_Climate_Change" title="Department of Energy and Climate Change">Department of Energy and Climate Change</a> suggests that hydrogen technologies have the potential to deliver UK transport with near-zero emissions whilst reducing dependence on imported oil and curtailment of renewable generation. However, the technologies face very difficult challenges, in terms of cost, performance and policy. <sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydrogen_safety">Hydrogen safety</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Hydrogen safety">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_safety" title="Hydrogen safety">Hydrogen safety</a></div>
<p>Hydrogen has one of the widest explosive/ignition mix range with air of all the gases with few exceptions such as <a href="/wiki/Acetylene" title="Acetylene">acetylene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silane" title="Silane">silane</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ethylene_oxide" title="Ethylene oxide">ethylene oxide</a>. That means that whatever the mix proportion between air and hydrogen, a hydrogen leak will most likely lead to an explosion, not a mere flame, when a flame or spark ignites the mixture. This makes the use of hydrogen particularly dangerous in enclosed areas such as tunnels or underground parking.<sup id="cite_ref-Utgitar_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Utgitar-80">[80]</a></sup> Pure hydrogen-oxygen flames burn in the <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> color range and are nearly invisible to the naked eye, so a <a href="/wiki/Flame_detector" title="Flame detector">flame detector</a> is needed to detect if a hydrogen leak is burning. Hydrogen is odorless and leaks cannot be detected by smell.</p>
<p>Hydrogen codes and standards are <a href="/wiki/Code_(law)" class="mw-redirect" title="Code (law)">codes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Technical_standards" class="mw-redirect" title="Technical standards">standards</a> for hydrogen <a href="/wiki/Fuel_cell_vehicle" title="Fuel cell vehicle">fuel cell vehicles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stationary_fuel_cell_applications" title="Stationary fuel cell applications">stationary fuel cell applications</a> and <a href="/wiki/Portable_fuel_cell_applications" class="mw-redirect" title="Portable fuel cell applications">portable fuel cell applications</a>. There are codes and standards for the safe handling and storage of hydrogen, for example the standard for the installation of stationary fuel cell power systems from the <a href="/wiki/National_Fire_Protection_Association" title="National Fire Protection Association">National Fire Protection Association</a>.</p>
<p>Codes and standards have repeatedly been identified as a major institutional barrier to deploying <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_technologies" title="Hydrogen technologies">hydrogen technologies</a> and developing a hydrogen economy. To enable the commercialization of hydrogen in consumer products, new model building codes and equipment and other technical standards are developed and recognized by federal, state, and local governments.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>One of the measures on the roadmap is to implement higher safety standards like early leak detection with <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sensor" title="Hydrogen sensor">hydrogen sensors</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> The Canadian Hydrogen Safety Program concluded that hydrogen fueling is as safe as, or safer than, <a href="/wiki/Compressed_natural_gas" title="Compressed natural gas">compressed natural gas</a> (CNG) fueling.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> The European Commission has funded the first higher educational program in the world in hydrogen safety engineering at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Ulster" class="mw-redirect" title="University of Ulster">University of Ulster</a>. It is expected that the general public will be able to use hydrogen technologies in everyday life with at least the same level of safety and comfort as with today's fossil fuels.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_concerns">Environmental concerns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Environmental concerns">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>There are many concerns regarding the environmental effects of the manufacture of hydrogen. Hydrogen is made either by <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis_of_water" title="Electrolysis of water">electrolysis of water</a>, or by <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel_reforming" class="mw-redirect" title="Fossil fuel reforming">fossil fuel reforming</a>. Reforming a fossil fuel leads to a higher emissions of carbon dioxide compared with direct use of the fossil fuel in an internal combustion engine. Similarly, if hydrogen is produced by electrolysis from fossil-fuel powered generators, increased carbon dioxide is emitted in comparison with direct use of the fossil fuel.</p>
<p>Using renewable energy source to generate hydrogen by electrolysis would require greater energy input than direct use of the renewable energy to operate electric vehicles, because of the extra conversion stages and losses in distribution. Hydrogen as transportation fuel, however, is mainly used for fuel cells that do not produce greenhouse gas emission, but water.</p>
<p>There have also been some concerns over possible problems related to hydrogen gas leakage.<sup id="cite_ref-Hbad_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hbad-84">[84]</a></sup> Molecular hydrogen leaks slowly from most containment vessels. It has been hypothesized that if significant amounts of hydrogen gas (H<sub>2</sub>) escape, hydrogen gas may, because of ultraviolet radiation, form <a href="/wiki/Free_radicals" class="mw-redirect" title="Free radicals">free radicals</a> (H) in the stratosphere. These free radicals would then be able to act as catalysts for <a href="/wiki/Ozone_depletion" title="Ozone depletion">ozone depletion</a>. A large enough increase in stratospheric hydrogen from leaked H<sub>2</sub> could exacerbate the depletion process. However, the effect of these leakage problems may not be significant. The amount of hydrogen that leaks today is much lower (by a factor of 10–100) than the estimated 10–20% figure conjectured by some researchers; for example, in <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, the leakage rate is only 0.1% (less than the natural gas leak rate of 0.7%).
