At most, such leakage would likely be no more than 1–2% even with widespread hydrogen use, using present technology.<sup id="cite_ref-Hbad_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hbad-84">[84]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Costs">Costs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Costs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In 2004, the production of unit of hydrogen fuel by steam reformation or electrolysis was approximately 3 to 6 times more expensive than the production of an equivalent unit of fuel from natural gas.<sup id="cite_ref-fsec.ucf.edu_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fsec.ucf.edu-87">[87]</a></sup> When evaluating costs, fossil fuels are generally used as the reference. The energy content of these fuels is not a product of human effort and so has no cost assigned to it. Only the extraction, refining, transportation and production costs are considered. On the other hand, the energy content of a unit of hydrogen fuel must be manufactured, and so has a significant cost, on top of all the costs of refining, transportation, and distribution. Systems which use renewably generated electricity more directly, for example in <a href="/wiki/Trolleybus" title="Trolleybus">trolleybuses</a>, or in <a href="/wiki/Battery_electric_vehicle" title="Battery electric vehicle">battery electric vehicles</a> may have a significant economic advantage because there are fewer conversion processes required between primary energy source and point of use.</p>
<p>The barrier to lowering the price of high purity hydrogen is a cost of more than 35 kWh of electricity used to generate each kilogram of hydrogen gas. Hydrogen produced by steam reformation costs approximately three times the cost of natural gas per unit of energy produced. This means that if natural gas costs $6/million BTU, then hydrogen will be $18/million BTU. Also, producing hydrogen from electrolysis with electricity at 5 cents/kWh will cost $28/million BTU — about 1.5 times the cost of hydrogen from natural gas. Note that the cost of hydrogen production from electricity is a linear function of electricity costs, so electricity at 10 cents/kWh means that hydrogen will cost $56/million BTU.<sup id="cite_ref-fsec.ucf.edu_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fsec.ucf.edu-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>Demonstrated advances in electrolyser and fuel cell technology by ITM Power <sup id="cite_ref-ITMpower_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ITMpower-88">[88]</a></sup> are claimed to have made significant in-roads into addressing the cost of electrolysing water to make hydrogen. Cost reduction would make hydrogen from off-grid renewable sources economic for refueling vehicles.</p>
<p>Hydrogen pipelines are more expensive<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> than even long-distance electric lines. Hydrogen is about three times bulkier in volume than natural gas for the same <a href="/wiki/Enthalpy" title="Enthalpy">enthalpy</a>. Hydrogen accelerates the cracking of steel (<a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_embrittlement" title="Hydrogen embrittlement">hydrogen embrittlement</a>), which increases maintenance costs, leakage rates, and material costs. The difference in cost is likely to expand with newer technology: wires suspended in air can use higher voltage with only marginally increased material costs, but higher pressure pipes require proportionally more material.</p>
<p>Setting up a hydrogen economy would require huge investments in the infrastructure to store and distribute hydrogen to vehicles. In contrast, <a href="/wiki/Battery_electric_vehicle" title="Battery electric vehicle">battery electric vehicles</a>, which are already publicly available, would not necessitate immediate expansion of the existing infrastructure for electricity transmission and distribution. Power plant capacity that now goes unused at night could be used for recharging electric vehicles. A study conducted by the Pacific Northwest National Laboratory for the US Department of Energy in December 2006 found that the idle off-peak grid capacity in the US would be sufficient to power 84% of all vehicles in the US if they all were immediately replaced with electric vehicles.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>Different production methods each have differing associated investment and marginal costs. The energy and feedstock could originate from a multitude of sources, i.e. natural gas, nuclear, solar, wind, biomass, coal, other fossil fuels, and geothermal.</p>
<dl>
<dt>Natural Gas at Small Scale</dt>
<dd>Uses steam reformation. Requires 15.9&#160;million cubic feet (450,000&#160;m<sup>3</sup>) of gas, which, if produced by small 500&#160;kg/day reformers at the point of dispensing (i.e., the filling station), would equate to 777,000 reformers costing $1&#160;trillion and producing 150&#160;million tons of hydrogen gas annually. Obviates the need for distribution infrastructure dedicated to hydrogen. $3.00 per <a href="/wiki/Gasoline_gallon_equivalent" title="Gasoline gallon equivalent">GGE</a> (Gallons of Gasoline Equivalent)</dd>
<dt>Nuclear</dt>
<dd>Provides energy for electrolysis of water. Would require 240,000&#160;tons of unenriched uranium — that's 2,000 600-megawatt power plants, which would cost $840&#160;billion, or about $2.50 per GGE.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></dd>
<dt>Solar</dt>
<dd>Provides energy for electrolysis of water. Would require 2,500&#160;kWh of sun per square meter, 113&#160;million 40-kilowatt systems, which would cost $22&#160;trillion, or about $9.50 per GGE.</dd>
<dt>Wind</dt>
<dd>Provides energy for electrolysis of water. At 7&#160;meters per second average wind speed, it would require 1&#160;million 2-MW wind turbines, which would cost $3&#160;trillion, or about $3.00 per GGE.</dd>
<dt>Biomass</dt>
<dd>Gasification plants would produce gas with steam reformation. 1.5&#160;billion tons of dry biomass, 3,300 plants which would require 113.4&#160;million acres (460,000&#160;km²) of farm to produce the biomass. $565&#160;billion in cost, or about $1.90 per GGE</dd>
<dt>Coal</dt>
<dd>FutureGen plants use coal gasification then steam reformation. Requires 1&#160;billion tons of coal or about 1,000 275-megawatt plants with a cost of about $500&#160;billion, or about $1 per GGE.</dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li>DOE Cost targets<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Examples_and_pilot_programs">Examples and pilot programs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Examples and pilot programs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Brno,_Autotec,_Mercedes_Citaro_na_palivov%C3%A9_%C4%8Dl%C3%A1nky_II.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Brno%2C_Autotec%2C_Mercedes_Citaro_na_palivov%C3%A9_%C4%8Dl%C3%A1nky_II.jpg/220px-Brno%2C_Autotec%2C_Mercedes_Citaro_na_palivov%C3%A9_%C4%8Dl%C3%A1nky_II.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Brno%2C_Autotec%2C_Mercedes_Citaro_na_palivov%C3%A9_%C4%8Dl%C3%A1nky_II.jpg/330px-Brno%2C_Autotec%2C_Mercedes_Citaro_na_palivov%C3%A9_%C4%8Dl%C3%A1nky_II.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Brno%2C_Autotec%2C_Mercedes_Citaro_na_palivov%C3%A9_%C4%8Dl%C3%A1nky_II.jpg/440px-Brno%2C_Autotec%2C_Mercedes_Citaro_na_palivov%C3%A9_%C4%8Dl%C3%A1nky_II.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="1024" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Brno,_Autotec,_Mercedes_Citaro_na_palivov%C3%A9_%C4%8Dl%C3%A1nky_II.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Mercedes-Benz_O530_Citaro" class="mw-redirect" title="Mercedes-Benz O530 Citaro">Mercedes-Benz O530 Citaro</a> powered by hydrogen fuel cells, in <a href="/wiki/Brno" title="Brno">Brno</a>, <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Several domestic <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">U.S.</a> <a href="/wiki/Automobile" class="mw-redirect" title="Automobile">automobile</a> manufactures have committed to develop vehicles using hydrogen. The distribution of hydrogen for the purpose of transportation is currently being tested around the world, particularly in <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hynor" class="mw-redirect" title="Hynor">Norway</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_link_network" title="Hydrogen link network">Denmark</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, <a href="/wiki/California_Hydrogen_Highway" title="California Hydrogen Highway">California</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Japan_hydrogen_fuel_cell_project&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Japan hydrogen fuel cell project (page does not exist)">Japan</a> and <a href="/wiki/BC_hydrogen_highway" title="BC hydrogen highway">Canada</a>, but the cost is very high.</p>
<p>Some hospitals have installed combined electrolyser-storage-fuel cell units for local emergency power. These are advantageous for emergency use because of their low maintenance requirement and ease of location compared to internal combustion driven generators.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a> has committed to becoming the world's first hydrogen economy by the year 2050.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> Iceland is in a unique position. Presently, it imports all the petroleum products necessary to power its automobiles and <a href="/wiki/Fishing_fleet" title="Fishing fleet">fishing fleet</a>. Iceland has large geothermal resources, so much that the local price of electricity actually is <i>lower</i> than the price of the hydrocarbons that could be used to produce that electricity.</p>
<p>Iceland already converts its surplus electricity into exportable goods and hydrocarbon replacements. In 2002, it produced 2,000&#160;tons of hydrogen gas by electrolysis, primarily for the production of <a href="/wiki/Anhydrous_ammonia" class="mw-redirect" title="Anhydrous ammonia">ammonia</a> (NH<sub>3</sub>) for fertilizer. Ammonia is produced, transported, and used throughout the world, and 90% of the cost of ammonia is the cost of the energy to produce it. Iceland is also developing an aluminium-smelting industry. Aluminium costs are driven primarily by the cost of the electricity to run the smelters. Either of these industries could effectively export all of Iceland's potential <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_electricity" class="mw-redirect" title="Geothermal electricity">geothermal electricity</a>.</p>
<p>Neither industry directly replaces hydrocarbons. <a href="/wiki/Reykjav%C3%ADk" title="Reykjavík">Reykjavík</a>, Iceland, had a small pilot fleet of city buses running on compressed hydrogen,<sup id="cite_ref-detnews_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-detnews-94">[94]</a></sup> and research on powering the nation's fishing fleet with hydrogen is under way. For more practical purposes, Iceland might process imported oil with hydrogen to extend it, rather than to replace it altogether.</p>
<p>The Reykjavík buses are part of a larger program, HyFLEET:CUTE,<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> operating hydrogen fueled buses in eight European cities. HyFLEET:CUTE buses were also operated in Beijing, China and Perth, Australia (see below). A pilot project demonstrating a hydrogen economy is operational on the <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norwegian</a> island of <a href="/wiki/Utsira" title="Utsira">Utsira</a>. The installation combines <a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">wind power</a> and hydrogen power. In periods when there is surplus wind energy, the excess power is used for generating hydrogen by <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">electrolysis</a>. The hydrogen is stored, and is available for power generation in periods when there is little wind.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/United_States_Hydrogen_Policy" title="United States Hydrogen Policy">United States has a hydrogen policy with several examples</a>. A joint venture between <a href="/wiki/NREL" class="mw-redirect" title="NREL">NREL</a> and <a href="/wiki/Xcel_Energy" title="Xcel Energy">Xcel Energy</a> is combining <a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">wind power</a> and hydrogen power in the same way in Colorado.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_and_Labrador_Hydro" title="Newfoundland and Labrador Hydro">Hydro</a> in <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="Newfoundland and Labrador">Newfoundland and Labrador</a> are converting the current <a href="/wiki/Wind-Diesel_Hybrid_Power_Systems" class="mw-redirect" title="Wind-Diesel Hybrid Power Systems">wind-diesel Power System</a> on the remote island of <a href="/wiki/Ramea" title="Ramea">Ramea</a> into a <a href="/wiki/Wind-Hydrogen_Hybrid_Power_Systems" class="mw-redirect" title="Wind-Hydrogen Hybrid Power Systems">Wind-Hydrogen Hybrid Power Systems</a> facility.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> A similar pilot project on <a href="/wiki/Stuart_Island_(Washington)" title="Stuart Island (Washington)">Stuart Island</a> uses <a href="/wiki/Solar_power" title="Solar power">solar power</a>, instead of <a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">wind power</a>, to generate electricity. When excess electricity is available after the batteries are full, hydrogen is generated by electrolysis and stored for later production of electricity by fuel cell.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">UK</a> started a fuel cell pilot program in January 2004, the program ran two Fuel cell buses on route&#160;25 in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> until December 2005, and switched to route RV1 until January 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> The Hydrogen Expedition is currently working to create a hydrogen fuel cell-powered ship and using it to circumnavigate the globe, as a way to demonstrate the capability of hydrogen fuel cells.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p>Western Australia's Department of Planning and Infrastructure operated three Daimler Chrysler Citaro fuel cell buses as part of its Sustainable Transport Energy for Perth Fuel Cells Bus Trial in Perth.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> The buses were operated by Path Transit on regular Transperth public bus routes. The trial began in September 2004 and concluded in September 2007. The buses' fuel cells used a proton exchange membrane system and were supplied with raw hydrogen from a BP refinery in Kwinana, south of Perth. The hydrogen was a byproduct of the refinery's industrial process. The buses were refueled at a station in the northern Perth suburb of Malaga.</p>
<p>The United Nations Industrial Development Organization (<a href="/wiki/UNIDO" class="mw-redirect" title="UNIDO">UNIDO</a>) and the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Energy_and_Natural_Resources_(Turkey)" title="Ministry of Energy and Natural Resources (Turkey)">Turkish Ministry of Energy and Natural Resources</a> have signed in 2003 a $40 million trust fund agreement for the creation of the <a href="/wiki/International_Centre_for_Hydrogen_Energy_Technologies" title="International Centre for Hydrogen Energy Technologies">International Centre for Hydrogen Energy Technologies</a> (UNIDO-ICHET) in <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a>, which started operation in 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> A hydrogen forklift, a hydrogen cart and a mobile house powered by renewable energies are being demonstrated in UNIDO-ICHET's premises. An uninterruptible power supply system has been working since April 2009 in the headquarters of <a href="/wiki/%C4%B0DO" title="İDO">Istanbul Sea Buses</a> company.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydrogen-using_alternatives_to_a_fully_distributive_hydrogen_economy">Hydrogen-using alternatives to a fully distributive hydrogen economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Hydrogen-using alternatives to a fully distributive hydrogen economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>For other energy alternatives, see</p>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Energy_development" title="Energy development">Energy development</a></div>
<p>Hydrogen is simply a method to store and transmit energy. Various alternative energy transmission and storage scenarios which begin with hydrogen production, but do not use it for all parts of the store and transmission infrastructure, may be more economic, in both near and far term. These include:</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ammonia_economy">Ammonia economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Ammonia economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>An alternative to gaseous <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> as an energy carrier is to bond it with <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> from the air to produce <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>, which can be easily liquefied, transported, and used (directly or indirectly) as a clean and <a href="/wiki/Ammonia_as_a_fuel" class="mw-redirect" title="Ammonia as a fuel">renewable fuel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydrogen_production_of_greenhouse-neutral_alcohol">Hydrogen production of greenhouse-neutral alcohol</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Hydrogen production of greenhouse-neutral alcohol">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Methanol_economy" title="Methanol economy">Methanol economy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_economy" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethanol economy">ethanol economy</a></div>
<p>The methanol economy is a synfuel production energy plan which may begin with hydrogen production. Hydrogen in a full "hydrogen economy" was initially suggested as a way to make <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a>, in non-polluting form, available to automobiles. However, a theoretical alternative to address the same problem is to produce hydrogen centrally and immediately use it to make liquid fuels from a CO<sub>2</sub> source. This would eliminate the requirement to transport and store the hydrogen. The source could be CO<sub>2</sub> that is produced by fuel-burning power plants. In order to be greenhouse-neutral, the source for CO<sub>2</sub> in such a plan would need to be from air, biomass, or other source of CO<sub>2</sub> which is already in, or to be released into, the air.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Direct_methanol_fuel_cell" title="Direct methanol fuel cell">Direct methanol fuel cells</a> are in commercial use, though as of August 2011 they are not efficient.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_electrical_grid_plus_synthetic_methanol_fuel_cells">The electrical grid plus synthetic methanol fuel cells</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: The electrical grid plus synthetic methanol fuel cells">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Carbon_neutral_fuel" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon neutral fuel">Carbon neutral fuel</a></div>
<p>Many of the hybrid strategies described above, using captive hydrogen to generate other more easily usable fuels, might be more effective than hydrogen-production alone. Short term energy storage (meaning the energy is used not long after it has been captured) may be best accomplished with battery or even ultracapacitor storage. Longer term energy storage (meaning the energy is used weeks or months after capture) may be better done with synthetic methane or alcohols, which can be stored indefinitely at relatively low cost, and even used directly in some type of fuel cells, for electric vehicles. These strategies dovetail well with the recent interest in <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_hybrid_electric_vehicle" class="mw-redirect" title="Plug-in hybrid electric vehicle">Plug-in Hybrid Electric Vehicles</a>, or PHEVs, which use a hybrid strategy of electrical and fuel storage for their energy needs. Hydrogen storage has been proposed by some<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2007)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> to be optimal in a narrow range of energy storage time, probably somewhere between a few days and a few weeks. This range is subject to further narrowing with any improvements in battery technology. It is always possible that some kind of breakthrough in hydrogen storage or generation could occur, but this is unlikely given that the physical and chemical limitations of the technical choices are fairly well understood.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Captive_hydrogen_synthetic_methane_production_.28SNG_synthetic_natural_gas.29">Captive hydrogen synthetic methane production (SNG synthetic natural gas)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hydrogen_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Captive hydrogen synthetic methane production (SNG synthetic natural gas)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In a similar way as with synthetic alcohol production, hydrogen can be used on site to directly (nonbiologically) produce greenhouse-neutral gaseous fuels. Thus, captive-hydrogen-mediated production of greenhouse-neutral <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> has been proposed (note that this is the reverse of the present method of acquiring hydrogen from natural methane, but one that does not require ultimate burning and release of fossil fuel carbon). Captive hydrogen (and carbon dioxide from, for example, CCS (Carbon Capture &amp; Storage)) may be used onsite to <i>synthesize</i> methane, using the <a href="/wiki/Sabatier_reaction" title="Sabatier reaction">Sabatier reaction</a>. This is about 60% efficient, and with the round trip reducing to 20 to 36% depending on the method of fuel utilization. This is even lower than hydrogen, but the storage costs drop by at least a factor of 3, because of methane's higher boiling point and higher energy density. Liquid methane has 3.2 times the energy density of liquid hydrogen and is easier to store compactly. Additionally, the pipe infrastructure (<a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a> pipelines) are already in place. Natural-gas-powered vehicles already exist, and are known to be easier to adapt from existing internal engine technology, than internal combustion autos running directly on hydrogen. Experience with natural gas powered vehicles shows that methane storage is inexpensive, once one has accepted the cost of conversion to store the fuel. However, the cost of alcohol storage is even lower, so this technology would need to produce methane at a considerable savings with regard to alcohol production. Ultimate mature prices of fuels in the competing technologies are not presently known, but both are expected to offer substantial infrastructural savings over attempts to transport and use hydrogen directly.</p>
<p>It has been proposed in a hypothetical renewable energy dominated energy system to use the excess electricity generated by wind, solar photovoltaic, hydro, marine currents and others to produce hydrogen by electrolysis of water then combine it with CO<sub>2</sub> make methane (natural gas).<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> Hydrogen would firstly be used onsite in fuel cells (CHP) or for transportation due to its greater efficiency of production and then methane created which could then be injected into the existing gas network to generate electricity and heat on demand to overcome low points of renewable energy production. The process described would be to create hydrogen (which could partly be used directly in fuel cells) and the addition of carbon dioxide CO<sub>2</sub> possibly from BECCS (Bio-Energy with Carbon Capture &amp; Storage <sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup>) via the (Sabatier reaction) to create methane as follows&#160;: CO<sub>2</sub> + 4H<sub>2</sub> → CH<sub>4</sub> + 2H<sub>2</sub>O.</p>
<p>Note: After combusting methane in CCGT the CO<sub>2</sub> would again be captured, i.e., CCS and used to produce new methane.</p>

<li id="cite_note-55"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-55">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a rel="nofollow" class="external free" href="http://www.nedstack.com/images/stories/news/documents/20120202_Press%20release%20Solvay%20PEM%20Power%20Plant%20start%20up.pdf">http://www.nedstack.com/images/stories/news/documents/20120202_Press%20release%20Solvay%20PEM%20Power%20Plant%20start%20up.pdf</a> Nedstack</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-zubrin-117-56"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-zubrin-117_56-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book"><a href="/wiki/Robert_Zubrin" title="Robert Zubrin">Zubrin, Robert</a> (2007). <i>Energy Victory</i>. Amherst, New York: Prometheus Books. pp.&#160;117–118. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-59102-591-7" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-59102-591-7">978-1-59102-591-7</a>. <q>The situation is much worse than this, however, because before the hydrogen can be transported anywhere, it needs to be either compressed or liquefied. To liquefy it, it must be refrigerated down to a temperature of -253°C (20 degrees above absolute zero). At these temperatures, fundamental laws of thermodynamics make refrigerators extremely inefficient. As a result, about 40 percent of the energy in the hydrogen must be spent to liquefy it. This reduces the actual net energy content of our product fuel to 792&#160;kcal. In addition, because it is a cryogenic liquid, still more energy could be expected to be lost as the hydrogen boils away as it is warmed by heat leaking in from the outside environment during transport and storage.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHydrogen+economy&amp;rft.aufirst=Robert&amp;rft.aulast=Zubrin&amp;rft.btitle=Energy+Victory&amp;rft.date=2007&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-59102-591-7&amp;rft.pages=117-118&amp;rft.place=Amherst%2C+New+York&amp;rft.pub=Prometheus+Books&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
